   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    North Pole
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       For other uses, see
       <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/North_Pole_(disambiguation)" title="North Pole (disambiguation)">
        North Pole (disambiguation)
       </a>
       .
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Not to be confused with
       <a href="/wiki/North_Magnetic_Pole" title="North Magnetic Pole">
        North Magnetic Pole
       </a>
       .
      </div>
      <p>
       <span style="font-size: small;">
        <span id="coordinates">
         <a href="/wiki/Geographic_coordinate_system" title="Geographic coordinate system">
          Coordinates
         </a>
         :
         <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion">
          <a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=North_Pole&amp;params=90_N_0_W_">
           <span class="geo-default">
            <span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">
             <span class="latitude">
              90°N
             </span>
             <span class="longitude">
              0°W
             </span>
            </span>
           </span>
           <span class="geo-multi-punct">
            ﻿ / ﻿
           </span>
           <span class="geo-nondefault">
            <span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">
             90°N -0°E
            </span>
            <span style="display:none">
             ﻿ /
             <span class="geo">
              90; -0
             </span>
            </span>
           </span>
          </a>
         </span>
        </span>
       </span>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:277px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arctic_Ocean.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="330" data-file-width="329" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Arctic_Ocean.png/275px-Arctic_Ocean.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fe/Arctic_Ocean.png 1.5x" width="275"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arctic_Ocean.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         An
         <a href="/wiki/Map_projection#Azimuthal_(projections_onto_a_plane)" title="Map projection">
          azimuthal projection
         </a>
         showing the Arctic Ocean and the North Pole. The map also shows the
         <a href="/wiki/75th_parallel_north" title="75th parallel north">
          75th parallel north
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/60th_parallel_north" title="60th parallel north">
          60th parallel north
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Noaa3-2006-0602-1206_(cropped).jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="720" data-file-width="1024" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Noaa3-2006-0602-1206_%28cropped%29.jpg/220px-Noaa3-2006-0602-1206_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Noaa3-2006-0602-1206_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Noaa3-2006-0602-1206_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Noaa3-2006-0602-1206_%28cropped%29.jpg/440px-Noaa3-2006-0602-1206_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Noaa3-2006-0602-1206_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Sea_ice" title="Sea ice">
          Sea ice
         </a>
         at the North Pole in 2006
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <b>
        North Pole
       </b>
       , also known as the
       <b>
        Geographic North Pole
       </b>
       or
       <b>
        Terrestrial North Pole
       </b>
       , is (subject to the caveats explained below) defined as the point in the
       <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">
        Northern Hemisphere
       </a>
       where the
       <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_rotation" title="Earth's rotation">
        Earth's axis of rotation
       </a>
       meets its surface.
      </p>
      <p>
       The North Pole is the northernmost point on the Earth, lying diametrically opposite the
       <a href="/wiki/South_Pole" title="South Pole">
        South Pole
       </a>
       . It defines geodetic
       <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">
        latitude
       </a>
       90° North, as well as the direction of
       <a href="/wiki/True_north" title="True north">
        true north
       </a>
       . At the North Pole all directions point south; all lines of
       <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">
        longitude
       </a>
       converge there, so its longitude can be defined as any degree value. Along tight latitude circles, counterclockwise is east and clockwise is west. The North Pole is at the center of the Northern Hemisphere.
      </p>
      <p>
       While the South Pole lies on a continental
       <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">
        land mass
       </a>
       , the North Pole is located in the middle of the
       <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Ocean" title="Arctic Ocean">
        Arctic Ocean
       </a>
       amid waters that are almost permanently covered with constantly shifting
       <a href="/wiki/Sea_ice" title="Sea ice">
        sea ice
       </a>
       . This makes it impractical to construct a permanent station at the North Pole (
       <a href="/wiki/Amundsen%E2%80%93Scott_South_Pole_Station" title="Amundsen–Scott South Pole Station">
        unlike the South Pole
       </a>
       ). However, the
       <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">
        Soviet Union
       </a>
       , and later Russia, constructed a number of
       <a href="/wiki/Drifting_ice_station" title="Drifting ice station">
        manned drifting stations
       </a>
       on a generally annual basis since 1937, some of which have passed over or very close to the Pole. Since 2002, the Russians have also annually established a base,
       <a href="/wiki/Barneo" title="Barneo">
        Barneo
       </a>
       , close to the Pole. This operates for a few weeks during early spring. Studies in the 2000s predicted that the North Pole may become seasonally ice-free because of
       <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_in_the_Arctic" title="Climate change in the Arctic">
        Arctic ice shrinkage
       </a>
       , with timescales varying from 2016
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       to the late 21st century or later.
      </p>
      <p>
       The sea depth at the North Pole has been measured at 4,261 m (13,980 ft) by the Russian
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MIR_(submersible)" title="MIR (submersible)">
        Mir submersible
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Arktika_2007" title="Arktika 2007">
        2007
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and at 4,087 m (13,410 ft) by
       <a href="/wiki/USS_Nautilus_(SSN-571)" title="USS Nautilus (SSN-571)">
        USS
        <i>
         Nautilus
        </i>
       </a>
       in 1958.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The nearest land is usually said to be
       <a href="/wiki/Kaffeklubben_Island" title="Kaffeklubben Island">
        Kaffeklubben Island
       </a>
       , off the northern coast of
       <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">
        Greenland
       </a>
       about 700 km (430 mi) away, though some perhaps semi permanent gravel banks lie slightly closer. The nearest permanently inhabited place is
       <a href="/wiki/Alert,_Nunavut" title="Alert, Nunavut">
        Alert
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Qikiqtaaluk_Region" title="Qikiqtaaluk Region">
        Qikiqtaaluk Region
       </a>
       , Nunavut, Canada, which is located 817 km (508 mi) from the Pole.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>
