      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Precise_definition">
        Precise definition
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Pole&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Precise definition">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Polar_motion" title="Polar motion">
        Polar motion
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Earth's axis of rotation – and hence the position of the North Pole – was commonly believed to be fixed (relative to the surface of the Earth) until, in the 18th century, the mathematician
       <a href="/wiki/Leonhard_Euler" title="Leonhard Euler">
        Leonhard Euler
       </a>
       predicted that the axis might "wobble" slightly. Around the beginning of the 20th century astronomers noticed a small apparent "variation of latitude," as determined for a fixed point on Earth from the observation of stars. Part of this variation could be attributed to a wandering of the Pole across the Earth's surface, by a range of a few metres. The wandering has several periodic components and an irregular component. The component with a period of about 435 days is identified with the eight-month wandering predicted by Euler and is now called the
       <a href="/wiki/Chandler_wobble" title="Chandler wobble">
        Chandler wobble
       </a>
       after its discoverer. The exact point of intersection of the Earth's axis and the Earth's surface, at any given moment, is called the "instantaneous pole", but because of the "wobble" this cannot be used as a definition of a fixed North Pole (or South Pole) when metre-scale precision is required.
      </p>
      <p>
       It is desirable to tie the system of Earth coordinates (latitude, longitude, and elevations or
       <a href="/wiki/Orography" title="Orography">
        orography
       </a>
       ) to fixed landforms. Of course, given
       <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">
        plate tectonics
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Isostasy" title="Isostasy">
        isostasy
       </a>
       , there is no system in which all geographic features are fixed. Yet the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Earth_Rotation_and_Reference_Systems_Service" title="International Earth Rotation and Reference Systems Service">
        International Earth Rotation and Reference Systems Service
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">
        International Astronomical Union
       </a>
       have defined a framework called the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Terrestrial_Reference_System" title="International Terrestrial Reference System">
        International Terrestrial Reference System
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration">
        Exploration
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Pole&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Exploration">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Arctic_exploration" title="Arctic exploration">
        Arctic exploration
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Farthest_North" title="Farthest North">
        Farthest North
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Arctic_expeditions" title="List of Arctic expeditions">
        List of Arctic expeditions
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_firsts_in_the_Geographic_North_Pole" title="List of firsts in the Geographic North Pole">
        List of firsts in the Geographic North Pole
       </a>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-1900">
        Pre-1900
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Pole&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Pre-1900">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mercator_north_pole_1595.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1606" data-file-width="1700" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Mercator_north_pole_1595.jpg/300px-Mercator_north_pole_1595.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Mercator_north_pole_1595.jpg/450px-Mercator_north_pole_1595.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Mercator_north_pole_1595.jpg/600px-Mercator_north_pole_1595.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mercator_north_pole_1595.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Gerardus_Mercator" title="Gerardus Mercator">
          Gerardus Mercator
         </a>
         's map of the North Pole from 1595
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:C.G._Zorgdragers_Bloeyende_opkomst_der_aloude_en_hedendaagsche_Groenlandsche_visschery_-_no-nb_digibok_2014010724007-V1.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2779" data-file-width="3721" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/C.G._Zorgdragers_Bloeyende_opkomst_der_aloude_en_hedendaagsche_Groenlandsche_visschery_-_no-nb_digibok_2014010724007-V1.jpg/220px-C.G._Zorgdragers_Bloeyende_opkomst_der_aloude_en_hedendaagsche_Groenlandsche_visschery_-_no-nb_digibok_2014010724007-V1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/C.G._Zorgdragers_Bloeyende_opkomst_der_aloude_en_hedendaagsche_Groenlandsche_visschery_-_no-nb_digibok_2014010724007-V1.jpg/330px-C.G._Zorgdragers_Bloeyende_opkomst_der_aloude_en_hedendaagsche_Groenlandsche_visschery_-_no-nb_digibok_2014010724007-V1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/C.G._Zorgdragers_Bloeyende_opkomst_der_aloude_en_hedendaagsche_Groenlandsche_visschery_-_no-nb_digibok_2014010724007-V1.jpg/440px-C.G._Zorgdragers_Bloeyende_opkomst_der_aloude_en_hedendaagsche_Groenlandsche_visschery_-_no-nb_digibok_2014010724007-V1.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:C.G._Zorgdragers_Bloeyende_opkomst_der_aloude_en_hedendaagsche_Groenlandsche_visschery_-_no-nb_digibok_2014010724007-V1.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         C.G. Zorgdragers map of the North Pole from 1720
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       As early as the 16th century, many prominent people correctly believed that the North Pole was in a sea, which in the 19th century was called the
       <a href="/wiki/Polynya" title="Polynya">
        Polynya
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Open_Polar_Sea" title="Open Polar Sea">
        Open Polar Sea
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was therefore hoped that passage could be found through ice floes at favorable times of the year. Several expeditions set out to find the way, generally with whaling ships, already commonly used in the cold northern latitudes.
      </p>
      <p>
       One of the earliest expeditions to set out with the explicit intention of reaching the North Pole was that of British naval officer
       <a href="/wiki/William_Parry_(explorer)" title="William Parry (explorer)">
        William Edward Parry
       </a>
       , who in 1827 reached latitude 82°45′ North. In 1871 the
       <a href="/wiki/Polaris_expedition" title="Polaris expedition">
        Polaris expedition
       </a>
       , a US attempt on the Pole led by
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_Francis_Hall" title="Charles Francis Hall">
        Charles Francis Hall
       </a>
       , ended in disaster. Another British
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">
        Royal Navy
       </a>
       attempt on the pole, part of the
       <a href="/wiki/British_Arctic_Expedition" title="British Arctic Expedition">
        British Arctic Expedition
       </a>
       , by Commander
       <a href="/wiki/Albert_Hastings_Markham" title="Albert Hastings Markham">
        Albert H. Markham
       </a>
       reached a then-record 83°20'26" North in May 1876 before turning back. An 1879–1881 expedition commanded by
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">
        US naval
       </a>
       officer
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_W._DeLong" title="George W. DeLong">
        George W. DeLong
       </a>
       ended tragically when their ship, the
       <a href="/wiki/USS_Jeannette_(1878)" title="USS Jeannette (1878)">
        USS
        <i>
         Jeanette
        </i>
       </a>
       , was crushed by ice. Over half the crew, including DeLong, were lost.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nansen-fram.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="273" data-file-width="415" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Nansen-fram.jpg/220px-Nansen-fram.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Nansen-fram.jpg/330px-Nansen-fram.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/56/Nansen-fram.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nansen-fram.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Nansen's ship
         <i>
          Fram
         </i>
         in the Arctic ice
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In April 1895 the Norwegian explorers
       <a href="/wiki/Fridtjof_Nansen" title="Fridtjof Nansen">
        Fridtjof Nansen
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Hjalmar_Johansen" title="Hjalmar Johansen">
        Hjalmar Johansen
       </a>
       struck out for the Pole on skis after leaving Nansen's icebound ship
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Fram" title="Fram">
         Fram
        </a>
       </i>
       . The pair reached latitude 86°14′ North before they abandoned the attempt and turned southwards, eventually reaching
       <a href="/wiki/Franz_Josef_Land" title="Franz Josef Land">
        Franz Josef Land
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1897 Swedish engineer
       <a href="/wiki/Salomon_August_Andr%C3%A9e" title="Salomon August Andrée">
        Salomon August Andrée
       </a>
       and two companions tried to reach the North Pole in the hydrogen balloon
       <i>
        Örnen
       </i>
       ("Eagle"), but came down 300 km (190 mi) north of
       <a href="/wiki/Kvit%C3%B8ya" title="Kvitøya">
        Kvitøya
       </a>
       , the northeasternmost part of the
       <a href="/wiki/Svalbard" title="Svalbard">
        Svalbard
       </a>
       archipelago. They trekked to Kvitøya but died there three months later. In 1930 the remains of
       <a href="/wiki/S._A._Andr%C3%A9e%27s_Arctic_Balloon_Expedition_of_1897" title="S. A. Andrée's Arctic Balloon Expedition of 1897">
        this expedition
       </a>
       were found by the Norwegian
       <a href="/wiki/Bratvaag_Expedition" title="Bratvaag Expedition">
        Bratvaag Expedition
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The Italian explorer
       <a href="/wiki/Prince_Luigi_Amedeo,_Duke_of_the_Abruzzi" title="Prince Luigi Amedeo, Duke of the Abruzzi">
        Luigi Amedeo, Duke of the Abruzzi
       </a>
       and Captain
       <a href="/wiki/Umberto_Cagni" title="Umberto Cagni">
        Umberto Cagni
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Regia_Marina" title="Regia Marina">
        Italian Royal Navy
       </a>
       (Regia Marina) sailed the converted whaler
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Jason_(ship)" title="Jason (ship)">
         Stella Polare
        </a>
       </i>
       ("Pole Star") from Norway in 1899. On 11 March 1900 Cagni led a party over the ice and reached latitude 86° 34’ on 25 April, setting a new record by beating Nansen's result of 1895 by 35 to 40 km (22 to 25 mi). Cagni barely managed to return to the camp, remaining there until 23 June. On 16 August the
       <i>
        Stella Polare
       </i>
       left
       <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Island" title="Rudolf Island">
        Rudolf Island
       </a>
       heading south and the expedition returned to Norway.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="1900.E2.80.9340">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="1900–40">
        1900–40
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Pole&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1900–40">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Peary_Sledge_Party_and_Flags_at_the_Pole.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2706" data-file-width="2034" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Peary_Sledge_Party_and_Flags_at_the_Pole.jpg/220px-Peary_Sledge_Party_and_Flags_at_the_Pole.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Peary_Sledge_Party_and_Flags_at_the_Pole.jpg/330px-Peary_Sledge_Party_and_Flags_at_the_Pole.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Peary_Sledge_Party_and_Flags_at_the_Pole.jpg/440px-Peary_Sledge_Party_and_Flags_at_the_Pole.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Peary_Sledge_Party_and_Flags_at_the_Pole.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Peary's sledge party at what they claimed was the North Pole, 1909. From left: Ooqueah, Ootah, Henson, Egingwah, Seeglo.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
          <a href="#cite_note-7">
           [7]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The US explorer
       <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Cook" title="Frederick Cook">
        Frederick Cook
       </a>
       claimed to have reached the North Pole on 21 April 1908 with two
       <a href="/wiki/Inuit" title="Inuit">
        Inuit
       </a>
       men, Ahwelah and Etukishook, but he was unable to produce convincing proof and his claim is not widely accepted.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The conquest of the North Pole was for many years credited to US Navy engineer
       <a href="/wiki/Robert_Peary" title="Robert Peary">
        Robert Peary
       </a>
       , who claimed to have reached the Pole on 6 April 1909, accompanied by
       <a href="/wiki/Matthew_Henson" title="Matthew Henson">
        Matthew Henson
       </a>
       and four Inuit men, Ootah, Seeglo, Egingwah, and Ooqueah. However, Peary's claim remains highly disputed and controversial. Those who accompanied Peary on the final stage of the journey were not trained in [Western] navigation, and thus could not independently confirm his navigational work, which some claim to have been particularly sloppy as he approached the Pole.
      </p>
      <p>
       The distances and speeds that Peary claimed to have achieved once the last support party turned back seem incredible to many people, almost three times that which he had accomplished up to that point. Peary's account of a journey to the Pole and back while traveling along the direct line – the only strategy that is consistent with the time constraints that he was facing – is contradicted by Henson's account of tortuous detours to avoid
       <a href="/wiki/Pressure_ridge_(ice)" title="Pressure ridge (ice)">
        pressure ridges
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Lead_(sea_ice)" title="Lead (sea ice)">
        open leads
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The British explorer
       <a href="/wiki/Wally_Herbert" title="Wally Herbert">
        Wally Herbert
       </a>
       , initially a supporter of Peary, researched Peary's records in 1989 and found that there were significant discrepancies in the explorer's navigational records. He concluded that Peary had not reached the Pole.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Support for Peary came again in 2005, however, when British explorer
       <a href="/wiki/Tom_Avery" title="Tom Avery">
        Tom Avery
       </a>
       and four companions recreated the outward portion of Peary's journey with replica wooden sleds and
       <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Eskimo_Dog" title="Canadian Eskimo Dog">
        Canadian Eskimo Dog
       </a>
       teams, reaching the North Pole in 36 days, 22 hours – nearly five hours faster than Peary. However, Avery's fastest 5-day march was 90 nautical miles, significantly short of the 135 claimed by Peary. Avery writes on his web site that "The admiration and respect which I hold for Robert Peary, Matthew Henson and the four Inuit men who ventured North in 1909, has grown enormously since we set out from
       <a href="/wiki/Cape_Columbia" title="Cape Columbia">
        Cape Columbia
       </a>
       . Having now seen for myself how he travelled across the pack ice, I am more convinced than ever that Peary did indeed discover the North Pole."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Papanin_with_flag.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="787" data-file-width="498" height="348" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Papanin_with_flag.jpg/220px-Papanin_with_flag.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Papanin_with_flag.jpg/330px-Papanin_with_flag.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Papanin_with_flag.jpg/440px-Papanin_with_flag.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Papanin_with_flag.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Papanin" title="Ivan Papanin">
          Ivan Papanin
         </a>
         at
         <a href="/wiki/North_Pole-1" title="North Pole-1">
          North Pole-1
         </a>
         drifting station, 1937
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Another rejection of Peary's claim arrived in 2009, when
       <a href="/wiki/E._Myles_Standish" title="E. Myles Standish">
        E. Myles Standish
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/California_Institute_of_Technology" title="California Institute of Technology">
        California Institute of Technology
       </a>
       , an experienced referee of scientific claims, reported numerous alleged
       <a href="/wiki/Lacuna_(manuscripts)" title="Lacuna (manuscripts)">
        lacunae
       </a>
       and inconsistencies.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DIOI_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-DIOI-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources">
          <span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (March 2016)">
           unreliable source?
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The first claimed flight over the Pole was made on 9 May 1926 by US naval officer
       <a href="/wiki/Richard_E._Byrd" title="Richard E. Byrd">
        Richard E. Byrd
       </a>
       and pilot
       <a href="/wiki/Floyd_Bennett" title="Floyd Bennett">
        Floyd Bennett
       </a>
       in a
       <a href="/wiki/Fokker_F.VII" title="Fokker F.VII">
        Fokker tri-motor
       </a>
       aircraft. Although verified at the time by a committee of the
       <a href="/wiki/National_Geographic_Society" title="National Geographic Society">
        National Geographic Society
       </a>
       , this claim has since been undermined
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       by the 1996 revelation that Byrd's long-hidden diary's solar
       <a href="/wiki/Sextant" title="Sextant">
        sextant
       </a>
       data (which the NGS never checked) consistently contradict his June 1926 report's parallel data by over 100 mi (160 km).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The secret report's alleged en-route solar sextant data were inadvertently so impossibly overprecise that he excised all these alleged raw solar observations out of the version of the report finally sent to geographical societies five months later (while the original version was hidden for 70 years), a realization first published in 2000 by the
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">
        University of Cambridge
       </a>
       after scrupulous refereeing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       According to Standish, "Anyone who is acquainted with the facts and has any amount of logical reasoning can not avoid the conclusion that neither Cook, nor Peary, nor Byrd reached the North Pole; and they all knew it."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DIOI_11-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-DIOI-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources">
          <span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (March 2016)">
           unreliable source?
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The first consistent, verified, and scientifically convincing attainment of the Pole was on 12 May 1926, by Norwegian explorer
       <a href="/wiki/Roald_Amundsen" title="Roald Amundsen">
        Roald Amundsen
       </a>
       and his US sponsor
       <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Ellsworth" title="Lincoln Ellsworth">
        Lincoln Ellsworth
       </a>
       from the
       <a href="/wiki/Airship" title="Airship">
        airship
       </a>
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Norge_(airship)" title="Norge (airship)">
         Norge
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        Norge
       </i>
       , though Norwegian-owned, was designed and piloted by the Italian
       <a href="/wiki/Umberto_Nobile" title="Umberto Nobile">
        Umberto Nobile
       </a>
       . The flight started from
       <a href="/wiki/Svalbard" title="Svalbard">
        Svalbard
       </a>
       in Norway, and crossed the Arctic Ocean to Alaska. Nobile, with several scientists and crew from the
       <i>
        Norge
       </i>
       , overflew the Pole a second time on 24 May 1928, in the airship
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Airship_Italia" title="Airship Italia">
         Italia
        </a>
       </i>
       . The
       <i>
        Italia
       </i>
       crashed on its return from the Pole, with the loss of half the crew.
      </p>
      <p>
       In May 1937 the world's first
       <a href="/wiki/Drifting_ice_station" title="Drifting ice station">
        North Pole ice station
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/North_Pole-1" title="North Pole-1">
        North Pole-1
       </a>
       , was established by Soviet scientists by air 20 kilometres (13 mi) from the North Pole. The expedition members: oceanographer
       <a href="/wiki/Pyotr_Shirshov" title="Pyotr Shirshov">
        Pyotr Shirshov
       </a>
       , meteorologist
       <a href="/wiki/Yevgeny_Fyodorov" title="Yevgeny Fyodorov">
        Yevgeny Fyodorov
       </a>
       , radio operator
       <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Krenkel" title="Ernst Krenkel">
        Ernst Krenkel
       </a>
       , and the leader
       <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Papanin" title="Ivan Papanin">
        Ivan Papanin
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       conducted scientific research at the station for the next nine months. By 19 February 1938, when the group was picked up by the ice breakers
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Icebreaker_Taymyr" title="Icebreaker Taymyr">
         Taimyr
        </a>
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Murman
       </i>
       , their station had drifted 2850 km to the eastern coast of Greenland.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="1940.E2.80.932000">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="1940–2000">
        1940–2000
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Pole&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1940–2000">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In May 1945 an
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">
        RAF
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Avro_Lancaster" title="Avro Lancaster">
        Lancaster
       </a>
       of the
       <i>
        Aries
       </i>
       expedition became the first
       <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">
        Commonwealth
       </a>
       aircraft to overfly the North Geographic and North Magnetic Poles. The plane was piloted by David Cecil McKinley of the
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">
        Royal Air Force
       </a>
       . It carried an 11-man crew, with Kenneth C. Maclure of the
       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Canadian_Air_Force" title="Royal Canadian Air Force">
        Royal Canadian Air Force
       </a>
       in charge of all scientific observations. In 2006, Maclure was honoured with a spot in
       <a href="/wiki/Canada%27s_Aviation_Hall_of_Fame" title="Canada's Aviation Hall of Fame">
        Canada's Aviation Hall of Fame
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Discounting Peary's disputed claim, the first men to set foot at the North Pole were a Soviet party
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       including geophysicists Mikhail Ostrekin and Pavel Senko, oceanographers Mikhail Somov and Pavel Gordienko,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and other scientists and flight crew (24 people in total)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Aleksandr_Kuznetsov_(explorer)" title="Aleksandr Kuznetsov (explorer)">
        Aleksandr Kuznetsov
       </a>
       's
       <i>
        Sever-2
       </i>
       expedition (March–May 1948).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was organized by the
       <a href="/wiki/Chief_Directorate_of_the_Northern_Sea_Route" title="Chief Directorate of the Northern Sea Route">
        Chief Directorate of the Northern Sea Route
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The party flew on three planes (pilots Ivan Cherevichnyy, Vitaly Maslennikov and Ilya Kotov) from
       <a href="/wiki/Kotelny_Island" title="Kotelny Island">
        Kotelny Island
       </a>
       to the North Pole and landed there at 4:44pm (
       <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Time" title="Moscow Time">
        Moscow Time
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/UTC%2B04:00" title="UTC+04:00">
        UTC+04:00
       </a>
       ) on 23 April 1948.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They established a temporary camp and for the next two days conducted scientific observations. On 26 April the expedition flew back to the continent.
      </p>
      <p>
       Next year, on 9 May 1949
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       two other Soviet scientists (Vitali Volovich and Andrei Medvedev)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       became the first people to parachute onto the North Pole.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They jumped from a
       <a href="/wiki/Douglas_C-47_Skytrain" title="Douglas C-47 Skytrain">
        Douglas C-47 Skytrain
       </a>
       , registered CCCP H-369.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On 3 May 1952
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">
        U.S. Air Force
       </a>
       Lieutenant Colonel
       <a href="/wiki/Joseph_O._Fletcher" title="Joseph O. Fletcher">
        Joseph O. Fletcher
       </a>
       and Lieutenant
       <a href="/wiki/William_Pershing_Benedict" title="William Pershing Benedict">
        William Pershing Benedict
       </a>
       , along with scientist
       <a href="/wiki/Albert_P._Crary" title="Albert P. Crary">
        Albert P. Crary
       </a>
       , landed a modified
       <a href="/wiki/Douglas_C-47_Skytrain" title="Douglas C-47 Skytrain">
        Douglas C-47 Skytrain
       </a>
       at the North Pole. Some Western sources considered this to be the first landing at the Pole
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       until the Soviet landings became widely known.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:USS_Skate_(SSN-578)_surfaced_in_Arctic_-_1959.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="524" data-file-width="750" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/USS_Skate_%28SSN-578%29_surfaced_in_Arctic_-_1959.jpg/220px-USS_Skate_%28SSN-578%29_surfaced_in_Arctic_-_1959.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/USS_Skate_%28SSN-578%29_surfaced_in_Arctic_-_1959.jpg/330px-USS_Skate_%28SSN-578%29_surfaced_in_Arctic_-_1959.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/USS_Skate_%28SSN-578%29_surfaced_in_Arctic_-_1959.jpg/440px-USS_Skate_%28SSN-578%29_surfaced_in_Arctic_-_1959.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:USS_Skate_(SSN-578)_surfaced_in_Arctic_-_1959.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/USS_Skate_(SSN-578)" title="USS Skate (SSN-578)">
          USS
          <i>
           Skate
          </i>
         </a>
         at drift station Alpha, 1958
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The United States Navy submarine
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/USS_Nautilus_(SSN-571)" title="USS Nautilus (SSN-571)">
         USS Nautilus
        </a>
       </i>
       (SSN-571) crossed the North Pole on 3 August 1958. On 17 March 1959
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/USS_Skate_(SSN-578)" title="USS Skate (SSN-578)">
         USS Skate
        </a>
       </i>
       (SSN-578) surfaced at the Pole, breaking through the ice above it, becoming the first naval vessel to do so.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Setting aside Peary's claim, the first confirmed surface conquest of the North Pole was that of
       <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Plaisted" title="Ralph Plaisted">
        Ralph Plaisted
       </a>
       , Walt Pederson, Gerry Pitzl and Jean Luc Bombardier, who traveled over the ice by
       <a href="/wiki/Snowmobile" title="Snowmobile">
        snowmobile
       </a>
       and arrived on 19 April 1968. The United States Air Force independently confirmed their position.
      </p>
      <p>
       On 6 April 1969
       <a href="/wiki/Wally_Herbert" title="Wally Herbert">
        Wally Herbert
       </a>
       and companions Allan Gill,
       <a href="/wiki/Roy_Koerner" title="Roy Koerner">
        Roy Koerner
       </a>
       and Kenneth Hedges of the British Trans-Arctic Expedition became the first men to reach the North Pole on foot (albeit with the aid of
       <a href="/wiki/Sled_dog" title="Sled dog">
        dog teams
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Airdrop" title="Airdrop">
        airdrops
       </a>
       ). They continued on to complete the first surface crossing of the Arctic Ocean – and by its longest axis,
       <a href="/wiki/Barrow,_Alaska" title="Barrow, Alaska">
        Barrow, Alaska
       </a>
       to
       <a href="/wiki/Svalbard" title="Svalbard">
        Svalbard
       </a>
       – a feat that has never been repeated.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardianobit_33-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-guardianobit-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Because of suggestions (later proven false) of Plaisted's use of air transport, some sources classify Herbert's expedition as the first confirmed to reach the North Pole over the ice surface by any means.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardianobit_33-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-guardianobit-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the 1980s Plaisted's pilots
       <a href="/wiki/Welland_Phipps" title="Welland Phipps">
        Weldy Phipps
       </a>
       and Ken Lee signed affidavits asserting that no such airlift was provided.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is also said that Herbert was the first person to reach the
       <a href="/wiki/Pole_of_inaccessibility" title="Pole of inaccessibility">
        pole of inaccessibility
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RIAN_archive_186141_Nuclear_icebreaker_Arktika.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1001" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/RIAN_archive_186141_Nuclear_icebreaker_Arktika.jpg/220px-RIAN_archive_186141_Nuclear_icebreaker_Arktika.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/RIAN_archive_186141_Nuclear_icebreaker_Arktika.jpg/330px-RIAN_archive_186141_Nuclear_icebreaker_Arktika.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/RIAN_archive_186141_Nuclear_icebreaker_Arktika.jpg/440px-RIAN_archive_186141_Nuclear_icebreaker_Arktika.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RIAN_archive_186141_Nuclear_icebreaker_Arktika.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Icebreaker
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arktika_(1972_nuclear_icebreaker)" title="Arktika (1972 nuclear icebreaker)">
          <i>
           Arktika
          </i>
         </a>
         , the first
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surface_ship" title="Surface ship">
          surface ship
         </a>
         to reach the North Pole.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       On 17 August 1977 the Soviet
       <a href="/wiki/Nuclear-powered_icebreaker" title="Nuclear-powered icebreaker">
        nuclear-powered icebreaker
       </a>
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arktika_(1972_nuclear_icebreaker)" title="Arktika (1972 nuclear icebreaker)">
         Arktika
        </a>
       </i>
       completed the first surface vessel journey to the North Pole.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1982
       <a href="/wiki/Ranulph_Fiennes" title="Ranulph Fiennes">
        Ranulph Fiennes
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_R._Burton" title="Charles R. Burton">
        Charles R. Burton
       </a>
       became the first people to cross the Arctic Ocean in a single season. They departed from Cape Crozier,
       <a href="/wiki/Ellesmere_Island" title="Ellesmere Island">
        Ellesmere Island
       </a>
       , on 17 February 1982 and arrived at the geographic North Pole on 10 April 1982. They travelled on foot and snowmobile. From the Pole, they travelled towards Svalbard but, due to the unstable nature of the ice, ended their crossing at the ice edge after drifting south on an ice floe for 99 days. They were eventually able to walk to their expedition ship
       <i>
        MV Benjamin Bowring
       </i>
       and boarded it on 4 August 1982 at position 80:31N 00:59W. As a result of this journey, which formed a section of the three-year
       <a href="/wiki/Transglobe_Expedition" title="Transglobe Expedition">
        Transglobe Expedition
       </a>
       1979–1982, Fiennes and Burton became the first people to complete a circumnavigation of the world via both North and South Poles, by surface travel alone. This achievement remains unchallenged to this day.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1985
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Edmund_Hillary" title="Sir Edmund Hillary">
        Sir Edmund Hillary
       </a>
       (the first man to stand on the summit of Mount Everest) and
       <a href="/wiki/Neil_Armstrong" title="Neil Armstrong">
        Neil Armstrong
       </a>
       (the first man to stand on the moon) landed at the North Pole in a small twin-engined ski plane.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
        <a href="#cite_note-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Hillary thus became the first man to stand at both poles and on the summit of Everest.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1986
       <a href="/wiki/Will_Steger" title="Will Steger">
        Will Steger
       </a>
       , with seven teammates, became the first to be confirmed as reaching the Pole by dogsled and without resupply.
      </p>
      <p>
       On 6 May 1986
       <a href="/wiki/USS_Archerfish_(SSN-678)" title="USS Archerfish (SSN-678)">
        USS
        <i>
         Archerfish
        </i>
        (SSN 678)
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/USS_Ray_(SSN-653)" title="USS Ray (SSN-653)">
        USS
        <i>
         Ray
        </i>
        (SSN 653)
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/USS_Hawkbill_(SSN-666)" title="USS Hawkbill (SSN-666)">
        USS
        <i>
         Hawkbill
        </i>
        (SSN-666)
       </a>
       surfaced at the North Pole, the first tri-submarine surfacing at the North Pole.
      </p>
      <p>
       On 21 April 1987
       <a href="/wiki/Shinji_Kazama" title="Shinji Kazama">
        Shinji Kazama
       </a>
       of Japan became the first person to reach the North Pole on a
       <a href="/wiki/Motorcycle" title="Motorcycle">
        motorcycle
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kazama_1_38-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kazama_1-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kazama_2_39-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kazama_2-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On 18 May 1987
       <a href="/wiki/USS_Billfish_(SSN-676)" title="USS Billfish (SSN-676)">
        USS
        <i>
         Billfish
        </i>
        (SSN 676)
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/USS_Sea_Devil_(SSN-664)" title="USS Sea Devil (SSN-664)">
        USS
        <i>
         Sea Devil
        </i>
        (SSN 664)
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/HMS_Superb_(S109)" title="HMS Superb (S109)">
        HMS
        <i>
         Superb
        </i>
        (S 109)
       </a>
       surfaced at the North Pole, the first international surfacing at the North Pole.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1988 a 13-man strong team (9 Soviets, 4 Canadians)
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet-Canadian_1988_Polar_Bridge_Expedition" title="Soviet-Canadian 1988 Polar Bridge Expedition">
        skied across the arctic
       </a>
       from Siberia to northern Canada. One of the Canadians,
       <a href="/wiki/Richard_Weber_(explorer)" title="Richard Weber (explorer)">
        Richard Weber
       </a>
       became the first person to reach the Pole from both sides of the Arctic Ocean.
      </p>
      <p>
       On 4 May 1990 Børge Ousland and Erling Kagge became the first explorers ever to reach the North Pole unsupported, after a 58-day ski trek from Ellesmere Island in Canada, a distance of 800 km.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On 7 September 1991 the German research vessel
       <a href="/wiki/RV_Polarstern" title="RV Polarstern">
        <i>
         Polarstern
        </i>
       </a>
       and the Swedish
       <a href="/wiki/Icebreaker" title="Icebreaker">
        icebreaker
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Oden_(1988_icebreaker)" title="Oden (1988 icebreaker)">
        <i>
         Oden
        </i>
       </a>
       reached the North Pole as the first conventional powered vessels.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Both scientific parties and crew took oceanographic and geological samples and had a common
       <a href="/wiki/Tug_of_war" title="Tug of war">
        tug of war
       </a>
       and a
       <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">
        football
       </a>
       game on an ice floe. Polarstern again reached the pole exactly 10 years later
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       with the
       <a href="/wiki/USCGC_Healy_(WAGB-20)" title="USCGC Healy (WAGB-20)">
        <i>
         Healy
        </i>
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1998, 1999, and 2000
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lada_Niva" title="Lada Niva">
        Lada Niva
       </a>
       Marshs (special very large wheeled versions made by BRONTO, Lada/Vaz's experimental product division) were driven to the North Pole.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The 1998 expedition was dropped by parachute and completed the track to the North Pole. The 2000 expedition departed from a Russian research base around 114 km from the Pole and claimed an average speed of 20–15 km/h in an average temperature of −30 °C.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">
        21st century
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Pole&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 21st century">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Charlotte_Northpole.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2336" data-file-width="3504" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Charlotte_Northpole.jpg/220px-Charlotte_Northpole.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Charlotte_Northpole.jpg/330px-Charlotte_Northpole.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Charlotte_Northpole.jpg/440px-Charlotte_Northpole.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Charlotte_Northpole.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/USS_Charlotte_(SSN-766)" title="USS Charlotte (SSN-766)">
          USS
          <i>
           Charlotte
          </i>
         </a>
         at the North Pole in 2005
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Commercial airliner flights on the
       <a href="/wiki/Polar_route" title="Polar route">
        Polar routes
       </a>
       may pass within viewing distance of the North Pole. For example, the flight from
       <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">
        Chicago
       </a>
       to
       <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">
        Beijing
       </a>
       may come close as latitude 89° N, though because of prevailing winds return journeys go over the
       <a href="/wiki/Bering_Strait" title="Bering Strait">
        Bering Strait
       </a>
       . In recent years journeys to the North Pole by air (landing by helicopter or on a runway prepared on the ice) or by icebreaker have become relatively routine, and are even available to small groups of tourists through
       <a href="/wiki/Adventure_travel" title="Adventure travel">
        adventure holiday
       </a>
       companies. Parachute jumps have frequently been made onto the North Pole in recent years. The temporary seasonal Russian camp of
       <a href="/wiki/Barneo" title="Barneo">
        Barneo
       </a>
       has been established by air a short distance from the Pole annually since 2002, and caters for scientific researchers as well as tourist parties. Trips from the camp to the Pole itself may be arranged overland or by helicopter.
      </p>
      <p>
       The first attempt at
       <a href="/wiki/Scuba_diving" title="Scuba diving">
        underwater
       </a>
       exploration of the North Pole was made on 22 April 1998 by Russian firefighter and diver Andrei Rozhkov with the support of the Diving Club of
       <a href="/wiki/Moscow_State_University" title="Moscow State University">
        Moscow State University
       </a>
       , but ended in fatality. The next attempted dive at the North Pole was organized the next year by the same diving club, and ended in success on 24 April 1999. The divers were Michael Wolff (Austria), Brett Cormick (UK), and Bob Wass (USA).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 2005 the United States Navy submarine
       <a href="/wiki/USS_Charlotte_(SSN-766)" title="USS Charlotte (SSN-766)">
        USS
        <i>
         Charlotte
        </i>
       </a>
       (SSN-766) surfaced through 155 cm (61 in) of ice at the North Pole and spent 18 hours there.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In July 2007 British endurance swimmer
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lewis_Gordon_Pugh" title="Lewis Gordon Pugh">
        Lewis Gordon Pugh
       </a>
       completed a 1 km (0.62 mi) swim at the North Pole. His feat, undertaken to highlight the effects of
       <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">
        global warming
       </a>
       , took place in clear water that had opened up between the ice floes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       His later attempt to paddle a
       <a href="/wiki/Kayak" title="Kayak">
        kayak
       </a>
       to the North Pole in late 2008, following the erroneous prediction of clear water to the Pole, was stymied when his expedition found itself stuck in thick ice after only three days. The expedition was then abandoned.
      </p>
      <p>
       By September 2007 the North Pole had been visited 66 times by different surface ships: 54 times by Soviet and Russian icebreakers, 4 times by Swedish
       <i>
        Oden
       </i>
       , 3 times by German
       <a href="/wiki/RV_Polarstern" title="RV Polarstern">
        <i>
         Polarstern
        </i>
       </a>
       , 3 times by
       <a href="/wiki/USCGC_Healy_(WAGB-20)" title="USCGC Healy (WAGB-20)">
        USCGC
        <i>
         Healy
        </i>
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USCGC_Polar_Sea" title="USCGC Polar Sea">
        USCGC
        <i>
         Polar Sea
        </i>
       </a>
       , and once by
       <a href="/wiki/CCGS_Louis_S._St-Laurent" title="CCGS Louis S. St-Laurent">
        CCGS
        <i>
         Louis S. St-Laurent
        </i>
       </a>
       and by Swedish
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Vidar_Viking" title="Vidar Viking">
         Vidar Viking
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="2007_descent_to_the_North_Pole_seabed">
        2007 descent to the North Pole seabed
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Pole&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 2007 descent to the North Pole seabed">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Arktika_2007" title="Arktika 2007">
        Arktika 2007
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mir_front.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2272" data-file-width="1704" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Mir_front.jpg/220px-Mir_front.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Mir_front.jpg/330px-Mir_front.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Mir_front.jpg/440px-Mir_front.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mir_front.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MIR_(submersible)" title="MIR (submersible)">
          MIR submersible
         </a>
         , one of the two vehicles that were used in
         <a href="/wiki/Arktika_2007" title="Arktika 2007">
          the first ever manned descent to the seabed under the North Pole
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       On 2 August 2007 a Russian scientific expedition
       <a href="/wiki/Arktika_2007" title="Arktika 2007">
        Arktika 2007
       </a>
       made the first ever manned descent to the ocean floor at the North Pole, to a depth of 4.3 km (2.7 mi), as part of the research programme in support of Russia's
       <a href="/wiki/Territorial_claims_in_the_Arctic#2001_Russian_claim" title="Territorial claims in the Arctic">
        2001 extended continental shelf claim
       </a>
       to a large swathe of the Arctic Ocean floor. The descent took place in two
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MIR_(submersible)" title="MIR (submersible)">
        MIR submersibles
       </a>
       and was led by Soviet and Russian polar explorer
       <a href="/wiki/Artur_Chilingarov" title="Artur Chilingarov">
        Artur Chilingarov
       </a>
       . In a symbolic act of visitation, the
       <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Russia" title="Flag of Russia">
        Russian flag
       </a>
       was placed on the ocean floor exactly at the Pole.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_50-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The expedition was the latest in a series of efforts intended to give Russia a dominant influence in the
       <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">
        Arctic
       </a>
       according to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_Times" title="New York Times">
        New York Times
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The warming
       <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_the_Arctic" title="Climate of the Arctic">
        Arctic climate
       </a>
       and summer shrinkage of the iced area has attracted the attention of many countries, such as China and the United States, toward the top of the world, where resources and shipping routes may soon be exploitable.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="MLAE_2009_Expedition">
        MLAE 2009 Expedition
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Pole&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: MLAE 2009 Expedition">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In 2009 the Russian
       <a href="/wiki/MLAE-2009" title="MLAE-2009">
        Marine Live-Ice Automobile Expedition (MLAE-2009)
       </a>
       with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vasily_Elagin" title="Vasily Elagin">
        Vasily Elagin
       </a>
       as a leader and a team of Afanasy Makovnev, Vladimir Obikhod, Alexey Shkrabkin, Sergey Larin, Alexey Ushakov and Nikolay Nikulshin reached the North Pole on two custom-built 6 x 6 low-pressure-tire ATVs — Yemelya-1 and Yemelya-2, designed by Vasily Elagin, a known Russian mountain climber, explorer and engineer. The vehicles reached the North Pole on 26 April 2009, 17:30 (Moscow time). The expedition was partly supported by Russian State Aviation. The Russian Book of Records recognized it as the first successful vehicle trip from land to the Geographical North Pole.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="MLAE_2013_Expedition">
        MLAE 2013 Expedition
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Pole&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: MLAE 2013 Expedition">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ML130310.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3840" data-file-width="5760" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/ML130310.JPG/220px-ML130310.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/ML130310.JPG/330px-ML130310.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/ML130310.JPG/440px-ML130310.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ML130310.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Yemelya — all terrain amphibious vehicle
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       On 1 March 2013 the Russian Marine Live-Ice Automobile Expedition (MLAE 2013) with Vasily Elagin as a leader, and a team of Afanasy Makovnev, Vladimir Obikhod, Alexey Shkrabkin, Andrey Vankov, Sergey Isayev and Nikolay Kozlov on two custom-built 6 x 6 low-pressure-tire ATVs — Yemelya-3 and Yemelya-4,— started from Golomyanny Island (the Severnaya Zemlya Archipelago) to the North Pole across drifting ice of the Arctic Ocean. The vehicles reached the Pole on 6 April and then continued to the Canadian coast. The coast was reached on 30 April 2013 (83°08N, 075°59W Ward Hant Island), and on 5 May 2013 the expedition finished in Resolute Bay, NU. The way between the Russian borderland (Machtovyi Island of the Severnaya Zemlya Archipelago, 80°15N, 097°27E) and the Canadian coast (Ward Hant Island, 83°08N, 075°59W) took 55 days; it was ~2300 km across drifting ice and about 4000 km in total. The expedition was totally self-dependent and used no external supplies. The expedition was supported by the
       <a href="/wiki/Russian_Geographical_Society" title="Russian Geographical Society">
        Russian Geographical Society
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yemelya.ru_54-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-yemelya.ru-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Day_and_night">
        Day and night
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Pole&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Day and night">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The sun at the North Pole is continuously
       <a href="/wiki/Midnight_sun" title="Midnight sun">
        above the horizon
       </a>
       during the summer and continuously
       <a href="/wiki/Polar_night" title="Polar night">
        below the horizon
       </a>
       during the winter.
       <a href="/wiki/Sunrise" title="Sunrise">
        Sunrise
       </a>
       is just before the
       <a href="/wiki/March_equinox" title="March equinox">
        March equinox
       </a>
       (around 20 March); the sun then takes three months to reach its highest point of near 23½° elevation at the summer
       <a href="/wiki/Solstice" title="Solstice">
        solstice
       </a>
       (around 21 June), after which time it begins to sink, reaching
       <a href="/wiki/Sunset" title="Sunset">
        sunset
       </a>
       just after the
       <a href="/wiki/September_equinox" title="September equinox">
        September equinox
       </a>
       (around 23 September). When the sun is visible in the polar sky, it appears to move in a horizontal circle above the horizon. This circle gradually rises from near the horizon just after the vernal equinox to its maximum elevation (in degrees) above the horizon at summer solstice and then sinks back toward the horizon before sinking below it at the autumnal equinox. Hence the North and South Poles experience the slowest rates of sunrise and sunset on Earth.
      </p>
      <p>
       A civil
       <a href="/wiki/Twilight" title="Twilight">
        twilight
       </a>
       period of about two weeks occurs before sunrise and after sunset, a nautical twilight period of about five weeks occurs before sunrise and after sunset and an astronomical twilight period of about seven weeks occurs before sunrise and after sunset.
      </p>
      <p>
       These effects are caused by a combination of the Earth's
       <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">
        axial tilt
       </a>
       and its revolution around the sun. The direction of the Earth's axial tilt, as well as its angle relative to the plane of the Earth's orbit around the sun, remains very nearly constant over the course of a year (both change very slowly over long time periods). At northern midsummer the North Pole is facing towards the sun to its maximum extent. As the year progresses and the Earth moves around the sun, the North Pole gradually turns away from the sun until at midwinter it is facing away from the Sun to its maximum extent. A similar sequence is observed at the South Pole, with a six-month time difference.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Time">
        Time
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Pole&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Time">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       In most places on Earth, local time is determined by longitude, such that the time of day is more-or-less synchronised to the position of the sun in the sky (for example, at midday the sun is roughly at its highest). This line of reasoning fails at the North Pole, where the sun rises and sets only once per year, and all lines of longitude, and hence all time zones, converge. There is no permanent human presence at the North Pole and no particular time zone has been assigned. Polar expeditions may use any time zone that is convenient, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Greenwich_Mean_Time" title="Greenwich Mean Time">
        Greenwich Mean Time
       </a>
       , or the time zone of the country from which they departed
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">
        Climate
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Pole&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Climate">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="451" data-file-width="540" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg/300px-2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg/450px-2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f0/2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2007_Arctic_Sea_Ice.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_in_the_Arctic" title="Climate change in the Arctic">
          Arctic ice shrinkages
         </a>
         of 2007 compared to 2005 and also compared to the 1979–2000 average.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_the_Arctic" title="Climate of the Arctic">
        Climate of the Arctic
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_in_the_Arctic" title="Climate change in the Arctic">
        Climate change in the Arctic
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The North Pole is substantially warmer than the
       <a href="/wiki/South_Pole" title="South Pole">
        South Pole
       </a>
       because it lies at sea level in the middle of an ocean (which acts as a reservoir of heat), rather than at altitude on a continental land mass. Despite being an ice cap, it shares some characteristics with a tundra climate (
       <i>
        ETf
       </i>
       ) due to the July and August temperatures peaking just above freezing.
      </p>
      <p>
       Winter temperatures at the North Pole can range from about −50 to −13 °C (−58 to 9 °F), averaging around −31 °C (−24 °F).
       <sup class="reference plainlinks nourlexpansion" id="ref_A">
        <a href="#endnote_A">
         A
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, a freak storm caused the temperature to reach 0.7 °C (33 °F) for a time at a
       <a href="/wiki/World_Meteorological_Organization" title="World Meteorological Organization">
        World Meteorological Organization
       </a>
       buoy, located at 87.45°N, on December 30, 2015. It was estimated that the temperature at the North Pole was between 30 and 35 °F (−1 and 2 °C) during the storm.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Summer temperatures (June, July, and August) average around the freezing point (0 °C (32 °F)). The highest temperature yet recorded is 13 °C (55 °F),
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weather_56-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-weather-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       much warmer than the South Pole's record high of only −12.3 °C (9.9 °F).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A similar spike in temperatures occurred on November 15, 2016 when temperatures hit freezing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The sea ice at the North Pole is typically around 2 to 3 m (6 ft 7 in to 9 ft 10 in) thick,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       although ice thickness, its spatial extent, and the fraction of open water within the ice pack can vary rapidly and profoundly in response to weather and climate.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Studies have shown that the average ice thickness has decreased in recent years.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is likely that
       <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">
        global warming
       </a>
       has contributed to this, but it is not possible to attribute the recent abrupt decrease in thickness entirely to the observed warming in the Arctic.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Reports have also predicted that within a few decades the Arctic Ocean will be entirely free of ice in the summer.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This may have significant commercial implications; see "Territorial Claims," below.
      </p>
      <p>
       The retreat of the Arctic sea ice will accelerate global warming, as less ice cover reflects less solar radiation, and may have serious climate implications by contributing to Arctic cyclone generation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable collapsible" style="width:90%; text-align:center; font-size:90%; line-height: 1.2em; margin:auto;">

        </td>
