      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Flora_and_fauna">
        Flora and fauna
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Pole&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Flora and fauna">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Polar_bear" title="Polar bear">
        Polar bears
       </a>
       are believed rarely to travel beyond about 82° North owing to the scarcity of food, though tracks have been seen in the vicinity of the North Pole, and a 2006 expedition reported sighting a polar bear just 1 mi (1.6 km) from the Pole.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Ringed_seal" title="Ringed seal">
        ringed seal
       </a>
       has also been seen at the Pole, and
       <a href="/wiki/Arctic_fox" title="Arctic fox">
        Arctic foxes
       </a>
       have been observed less than 60 km (37 mi) away at 89°40′ N.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
        <a href="#cite_note-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Birds seen at or very near the Pole include the
       <a href="/wiki/Snow_bunting" title="Snow bunting">
        snow bunting
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Northern_fulmar" title="Northern fulmar">
        northern fulmar
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Black-legged_kittiwake" title="Black-legged kittiwake">
        black-legged kittiwake
       </a>
       , though some bird sightings may be distorted by the tendency of birds to follow ships and expeditions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-calgary_69-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-calgary-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Fish have been seen in the waters at the North Pole, but these are probably few in number.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-calgary_69-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-calgary-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A member of the Russian team that descended to the North Pole seabed in August 2007 reported seeing no sea creatures living there.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_50-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, it was later reported that a
       <a href="/wiki/Sea_anemone" title="Sea anemone">
        sea anemone
       </a>
       had been scooped up from the seabed mud by the Russian team and that video footage from the dive showed unidentified
       <a href="/wiki/Shrimp" title="Shrimp">
        shrimps
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Amphipoda" title="Amphipoda">
        amphipods
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
        <a href="#cite_note-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Territorial_claims_to_the_North_Pole_and_Arctic_regions">
        Territorial claims to the North Pole and Arctic regions
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Pole&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Territorial claims to the North Pole and Arctic regions">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Territorial_claims_in_the_Arctic" title="Territorial claims in the Arctic">
        Territorial claims in the Arctic
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sunset_over_the_North_Pole_at_the_International_Date_line_at_20,000_feet_Aug_6th_2015_by_D_Ramey_Logan.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4496" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Sunset_over_the_North_Pole_at_the_International_Date_line_at_20%2C000_feet_Aug_6th_2015_by_D_Ramey_Logan.JPG/220px-Sunset_over_the_North_Pole_at_the_International_Date_line_at_20%2C000_feet_Aug_6th_2015_by_D_Ramey_Logan.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Sunset_over_the_North_Pole_at_the_International_Date_line_at_20%2C000_feet_Aug_6th_2015_by_D_Ramey_Logan.JPG/330px-Sunset_over_the_North_Pole_at_the_International_Date_line_at_20%2C000_feet_Aug_6th_2015_by_D_Ramey_Logan.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Sunset_over_the_North_Pole_at_the_International_Date_line_at_20%2C000_feet_Aug_6th_2015_by_D_Ramey_Logan.JPG/440px-Sunset_over_the_North_Pole_at_the_International_Date_line_at_20%2C000_feet_Aug_6th_2015_by_D_Ramey_Logan.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sunset_over_the_North_Pole_at_the_International_Date_line_at_20,000_feet_Aug_6th_2015_by_D_Ramey_Logan.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Sunset over the North Pole at the
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Dateline" title="International Dateline">
          International Dateline
         </a>
         , 2015
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Currently, under
       <a href="/wiki/International_law" title="International law">
        international law
       </a>
       , no country owns the North Pole or the region of the Arctic Ocean surrounding it. The five surrounding Arctic countries, Russian Federation, Canada, Norway, Denmark (via Greenland), and the United States (via Alaska), are limited to a 200-
       <a href="/wiki/Nautical_mile" title="Nautical mile">
        nautical-mile
       </a>
       (370 km; 230 mi)
       <a href="/wiki/Exclusive_economic_zone" title="Exclusive economic zone">
        exclusive economic zone
       </a>
       around their coasts, and the area beyond that is administered by the
       <a href="/wiki/International_Seabed_Authority" title="International Seabed Authority">
        International Seabed Authority
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Upon ratification of the
       <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Convention_on_the_Law_of_the_Sea" title="United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea">
        United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea
       </a>
       , a country has 10 years to make claims to an extended continental shelf beyond its 200-mile exclusive economic zone. If validated, such a claim gives the claimant state rights to what may be on or beneath the sea bottom within the claimed zone.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Norway (ratified the convention in 1996
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ratif_72-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ratif-72">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ), Russia (ratified in 1997
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ratif_72-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ratif-72">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ), Canada (ratified in 2003
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ratif_72-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-ratif-72">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ) and Denmark (ratified in 2004
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ratif_72-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-ratif-72">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ) have all launched projects to base claims that certain areas of Arctic continental shelves should be subject to their sole sovereign exploitation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
        <a href="#cite_note-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1907 Canada invoked a "sector principle" to claim sovereignty over a sector stretching from its coasts to the North Pole. This claim has not been relinquished, but was not consistently pressed until 2013.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
        <a href="#cite_note-75">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_associations">
        Cultural associations
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Pole&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Cultural associations">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       In some children's
       <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">
        Western cultures
       </a>
       , the geographic North Pole is described as the location of
       <a href="/wiki/Santa%27s_workshop" title="Santa's workshop">
        Santa Claus' workshop and residence
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Legends_77-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Legends-77">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       although the depictions have been inconsistent between the geographic and magnetic North Pole.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2014)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Canada_Post" title="Canada Post">
        Canada Post
       </a>
       has assigned postal code
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H0H_0H0" title="H0H 0H0">
        H0H 0H0
       </a>
       to the North Pole (referring to Santa's traditional exclamation of "
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ho_ho_ho" title="Ho ho ho">
        Ho ho ho
       </a>
       !").
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
        <a href="#cite_note-79">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       This association reflects an age-old esoteric mythology of
       <a href="/wiki/Hyperborea" title="Hyperborea">
        Hyperborea
       </a>
       that posits the North Pole, the otherworldly world-axis, as the abode of God and superhuman beings.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The popular figure of the pole-dwelling Santa Claus thus functions as an archetype of spiritual purity and transcendence.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
        <a href="#cite_note-81">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       As
       <a href="/wiki/Henry_Corbin" title="Henry Corbin">
        Henry Corbin
       </a>
       has documented, the North Pole plays a key part in the cultural worldview of
       <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">
        Sufism
       </a>
       and Iranian mysticism. "The Orient sought by the mystic, the Orient that cannot be located on our maps, is in the direction of the north, beyond the north."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Owing to its remoteness, the Pole is sometimes identified with a mysterious mountain of ancient
       <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Iran" title="Culture of Iran">
        Iranian
       </a>
       tradition called
       <a href="/wiki/Mount_Qaf" title="Mount Qaf">
        Mount Qaf
       </a>
       (Jabal Qaf), the "farthest point of the earth".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
        <a href="#cite_note-84">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to certain authors, the Jabal Qaf of
       <a href="/wiki/Cosmology_in_medieval_Islam" title="Cosmology in medieval Islam">
        Muslim cosmology
       </a>
       is a version of
       <a href="/wiki/Rupes_Nigra" title="Rupes Nigra">
        Rupes Nigra
       </a>
       , a mountain whose ascent, like
       <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">
        Dante's
       </a>
       climbing of the
       <a href="/wiki/Purgatorio" title="Purgatorio">
        Mountain of Purgatory
       </a>
       , represents the pilgrim's progress through spiritual states.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
        <a href="#cite_note-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In Iranian theosophy, the heavenly Pole, the focal point of the spiritual ascent, acts as a magnet to draw beings to its "palaces ablaze with immaterial matter."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
        <a href="#cite_note-86">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">
        See also
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">

         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">
        Notes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Pole&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Notes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <small>
         A.
         <span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_A">
          <b>
           <a href="#ref_A">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         Data is from a Greenlandic Weather Station at
         <span class="plainlinks nourlexpansion">
          <a class="external text" href="//tools.wmflabs.org/geohack/geohack.php?pagename=North_Pole&amp;params=83_38_N_033_22_W_type:landmark_scale:30000_region:GL&amp;title=Greenlandic+Weather+Station">
           <span class="geo-default">
            <span class="geo-dms" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">
             <span class="latitude">
              83°38′N
             </span>
             <span class="longitude">
              033°22′W
             </span>
            </span>
           </span>
           <span class="geo-multi-punct">
            ﻿ / ﻿
           </span>
           <span class="geo-nondefault">
            <span class="vcard">
             <span class="geo-dec" title="Maps, aerial photos, and other data for this location">
              83.633°N 33.367°W
             </span>
             <span style="display:none">
              ﻿ /
              <span class="geo">
               83.633; -33.367
              </span>
             </span>
             <span style="display:none">
              ﻿ (
              <span class="fn org">
               Greenlandic Weather Station
              </span>
              )
             </span>
            </span>
           </span>
          </a>
         </span>
         located 709 km (441 mi) from the North Pole.
        </small>
       </dd>
      </dl>

            ^

          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          <a class="external text" href="http://library.osu.edu/sites/archives/polar/flight/controversy.php" rel="nofollow">
           The North Pole Flight of Richard E. Byrd: An Overview of the Controversy
          </a>
          <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20071013144532/http://library.osu.edu/sites/archives/polar/flight/controversy.php" rel="nofollow">
           Archived
          </a>
          13 October 2007 at the
          <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">
           Wayback Machine
          </a>
          ., Byrd Polar Research Center of The Ohio State University. See also
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.dioi.org/vols/wa0.pdf" rel="nofollow">
           <i>
            DIO
           </i>
          </a>
          Vol. 10 [2000] (refereed both at University of Cambridge and by the
          <i>
           DIO
          </i>
          board), which reveals errors of grade school arithmetic in the Byrd-defenses of W.Molett (pp. 55 &amp; 98) and consultant J. Portney (pp. 73–75), neither of whom attempts to explain Byrd's surgical censoring of his original June report, or his and the National Geographic's hiding of said report for decades. Similarly, Avery's chimeral try at replicating the Peary 1909 trip via 2005 ice, may divert from but cannot explain Peary's data-blanks, data-alterations, nor why he, when reading his diary to Congress on 7 January 1911, understandably
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.dioi.org/cot.htm#wpst" rel="nofollow">
           deleted
          </a>
          (only) its sole attempt at explaining (crudely and inadequately) his steering: "setting course by moon, our shadows etc". See
          <i>
           The Washington Post
          </i>
          20 April 1989. Compare diary 2 April 1909 to p. 302 of the Peary Hearings: complete verbatim copy at 1916
          <i>
           Congressional Record
          </i>
          Vol. 53, Appendix pp. 293–327.
         </span>

           <a href="#cite_ref-13">

         <span class="reference-text">

        <li id="cite_note-14">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-14">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          D. Rawlins
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/Polar_Record" title="Polar Record">
            Polar Record
           </a>
          </i>
          (Scott Polar Research Institute) vol. 36 pp. 25–50. SPRI's preface: the paper "is considered to be of such significance to the community that it has been published here despite an expanded version being published this same month in
          <i>
           DIO
          </i>
          ." Both versions (p. 38 and 59, respectively) note that while Byrd's New York ticker-tape parade and his National Geographic Society gold medal presentation were on 23 June 1926, the NGS exam of his later-hidden original report was from early 23 June through late 28 June (six days, mistakenly cited as "five consecutive days" in the report), a chronology so revealing that the September
          <i>
           National Geographic
          </i>
          pp. 384–385 stripped out the dates (only) from the NGS' own report, which fortunately was published uncensored (thanks to the Secretary of the Navy) at
          <i>
           The New York Times
          </i>
          30 June, p. 5.
